<pstyle:Gallery 2 deck small><cs:21.000000>T<cs:><cs:21.000000><ck:-10>h<cs:><ck:><cs:21.000000>e<cs:><cs:21.000000><ck:-70> <cs:><ck:><cs:21.000000>A<cs:><cs:21.000000><ck:-30>n<cs:><ck:><cs:21.000000><ck:-10>ne<cs:><ck:><cs:21.000000><ck:-40>n<cs:><ck:><cs:21.000000>b<cs:><cs:21.000000><ck:-10>e<cs:><ck:><cs:21.000000>rg<0x000A>C<cs:><cs:21.000000><ck:-20>ou<cs:><ck:><cs:21.000000><ck:40>r<cs:><ck:><cs:21.000000><ck:30>t<cs:><ck:><cs:21.000000><ck:-30>y<cs:><ck:><cs:21.000000>ard<cs:>
<pstyle:Gallery introduction><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>Ryan Gander <cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><ccase:Small Caps><cl:9.250000>ra<cs:><ccase:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000><0x2019>s large and striking inflatable balls are part of an ongoing exploration by the artist that examines the world's fascination with having definitive answers. Each sphere is inscribed with an absurd question that represents the inquisitiveness of children, who ask what grown-up minds often dismiss as nonsensical or illogical. The work invokes the spirit of childhood and draws on Gander<0x2019>s use of children<0x2019>s toys as symbols of their imaginative powers.
<cs:><cl:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>The balls appear to have been playfully rolled into the Annenberg Courtyard, landing where they may, and immediately interacting with the architecture of Burlington House. Their enormity almost becomes a physical manifestation of the unanswerable, challenging the viewer <0x000A>to confront them and reflect. As the first work we encounter at this year<0x2019>s Summer Exhibition, Gander<0x2019>s installation provides a perfect introduction to the show<0x2019>s overall <0x000A>theme of <cs:><cl:><ct:Light Italic><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>Dialogues<ct:><cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>, which hopes to open up channels of exchange and engagement through art and architecture.<cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.350000>

<cl:><clang:><ch:><cstyle:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>The Wohl Central Hall is the first of several galleries hung collaboratively by members of the Summer Exhibition Committee <0x2013> a decision made in the spirit of this year<0x2019>s theme, Dialogues, to foster conversation during the hanging process. Here Co-ordinator Farshid Moussavi <cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><ccase:Small Caps><cl:9.250000>ra<cs:><ccase:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000> and Caragh Thuring <cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><ccase:Small Caps><cl:9.250000>ra<cs:><ccase:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000> have worked together to create a dramatic and unexpected monochromatic entrance to the exhibition.
<cs:><cl:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>Alice Channer<0x2019>s <cs:><cl:><ct:Light Italic><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>Body Shop<ct:><cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000> occupies the central space in the gallery. The installation was developed following a visit by the artist to a luxury car factory where she learned that within this very sterile and masculine environment, female ostrich feathers are used to remove dust particles between applications of paint <0x2013> an example of how this highly industrialised process still depends on nature. This is <0x000A>one of many suspended works throughout the show that interact with the architecture of the galleries.
<cs:><cl:><praon:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>Linking the Courtyard to the galleries, a sixth ball by Ryan Gander <cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><ccase:Small Caps><cl:9.250000>ra<cs:><ccase:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000> can be found wedged in the doorway leading to Gallery VI. Adorning the surrounding walls are prints and paintings in black and white that have been carefully grouped together, including works by Royal Academicians Rebecca Salter, Cornelia Parker and Tracey Emin, and a 4-metre-high print by Helen Sear <cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><ccase:Small Caps><cl:9.250000>ra<cs:><ccase:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>.
<cs:><cl:><praon:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>There are also works in memoriam to the late Norman Ackroyd <cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><ccase:Small Caps><cl:9.250000>ra<cs:><ccase:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>, including <cs:><cl:><ct:Light Italic><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>Windermere on 27th January<ct:><cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>, the largest etching ever produced by the master printmaker.<cs:><cl:>

<cstyle:><pstyle:Gallery 2 deck small><cs:21.000000><cbs:1.000000>Lecture<cs:><cbs:> <cstyle:Works numbers by text>works 105-199<0x000A><cstyle:><cs:21.000000><cbs:1.000000>Room<cs:><cbs:>
<pstyle:Gallery introduction><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>The Lecture Room has been hung by Royal Academicians Farshid Moussavi, Stephanie Macdonald and Tom Emerson, and Sir Christopher Le Brun <cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><ccase:Small Caps><cl:9.250000>ppra<cs:><ccase:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>.
<cs:><cl:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>The striking red backdrop to the gallery was specially made for the exhibition by invited artist Antonio Tarsis. The suspended assemblage is constructed from matchboxes from the artist<0x2019>s hometown of Salvador, Brazil, which have been carefully deconstructed and reformed to create an arresting, textured composition that provides a powerful visual narrative about survival, resilience and cultural identity.
<cs:><cl:><praon:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>On the walls, large-scale works by Academicians Sir Michael Craig-Martin, Shirazeh Houshiary and Stephen Chambers pick up hues of red around the room, as do the sculptural works by Ron Arad <cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><ccase:Small Caps><cl:9.250000>ra<cs:><ccase:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000> and David Nash <cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><ccase:Small Caps><cl:9.250000>ra<cs:><ccase:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>. 
<cs:><cl:><praon:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>For the first time in the Summer Exhibition, architecture can be found throughout the galleries, acknowledging its place within broader cultural conversations. Central to the gallery is Barkow Leibinger<0x2019>s dramatic set design model for the opera <cs:><cl:><ct:Light Italic><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>Fidelio<ct:><cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>. Surrounding it are large-scale models by Academicians Thomas Heatherwick, Sir Nicholas Grimshaw <cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><ccase:Small Caps><cl:9.250000>ppra<cs:><ccase:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000> and Lord Foster of Thames Bank. 
<cs:><cl:><praon:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>To display architectural models and three-dimensional objects, the committee wanted to move away from traditional white plinths and use a sustainable material instead. They decided on hemp blocks, which are increasingly being used in the construction business for their versatility and renewable nature. All undamaged bricks will be returned to The Hemp Block Company at the end of the show.

<cs:><cl:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>Central to the gallery is a wall of urban photography <0x000A>that includes a large-scale linocut of detritus by Andy Parker and a chromogenic print of a warm embrace by Baud Postma.
<cs:><cl:><praon:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>On the surrounding walls, a common thread linking many works is figures in movement, whether dancing, marching, protesting or trying to survive. <ctk:><ccase:><cl:><clang:><ch:><cstyle:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>Even with architecture spread throughout the galleries, Stephanie Macdonald<cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><ccase:Small Caps><cl:9.250000> ra<cs:><ccase:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000> and Tom Emerson<cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><ccase:Small Caps><cl:9.250000> ra<cs:><ccase:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000> from 6a architects knew that the discipline would hold a strong presence in their selection. They chose to focus their hang on the reconnection of nature and culture within architecture, something lost in the age of modernism, and they have drawn on a rich mix of mediums to explore these ideas.
<cs:><cl:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>Photography by invited artists Richard Wentworth and Juergen Teller joins multi-layered gicl<0x00E9>e prints by the architect and artist Arinjoy Sen. Victoria Morton<0x2019>s gestural paintings bring a softness that hints at the experiential aspect of architecture, and sculptures by Academicians Veronica Ryan and Assemble speak of its materiality. 
<cs:><cl:><praon:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>In their dynamic hang there are clusters of works that <0x000A>interact to create narratives with each other. For example, architectural details found in Boyd & Evans<0x2019>s painting <0x000A><cs:><cl:><ct:Light Italic><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>What Are We Waiting For?<ct:><cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000> are echoed in the nearby photography of architecture practice Pricegore. It is through connections such as these that Macdonald and Emerson hope to demonstrate how architecture is already deeply embedded in the artistic landscape.
<cs:><cl:><praon:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>A screen designed for an East London caf<0x00E9> by architect Shahed Saleem is decorated with cut-outs of historic objects connected to shipping and ship building in the Docklands, and a dramatic photograph from DnA_Design and Architecture shows how the practice has transformed a stone quarry into a cultural space.

<cstyle:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>It was Caragh Thuring<cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><ccase:Small Caps><cl:9.250000> ra<cs:><ccase:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000><0x2019>s aim in Gallery V to reflect the abiding concerns of this year<0x2019>s open submission entries <0x2013> which she calls <0x2018>a litmus test of the current state of the nation<0x2019>. Working as if the walls were a painting, Thuring took an intuitive approach to her hang, carefully using space to create something penetrable that people could digest.
<cs:><cl:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>One of the themes in the gallery is individuals<0x2019> human concerns and experiences in relation to their environment, including invited artist Michael Fullerton<0x2019>s luminous portraits of refugees who resided at the Hilltop Hotel, Carlisle. Other works tackle technology, boundaries and territories, and the effects of war and what it sets in motion, including a drawing by Fiona Banner <cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><ccase:Small Caps><cl:9.250000>ra<cs:><ccase:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>.
<cs:><cl:><praon:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>Anya Gallaccio presents cast bronzes of potatoes and artichokes, and her hand-knotted hop twine stretches between two galleries, creating dialogue between the spaces. Elsewhere there hangs a large unstretched canvas by the Argentine-Swiss artist Vivian Suter.
<cs:><cl:><praon:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>Royal Academicians Gary Hume and Adam Caruso and <0x000A>Peter St John have collaborated on an installation in response to the theme of Dialogues, in which the architects have conceptualised a model of a building to house the painter<0x2019>s self-portrait.
<cs:><cl:><praon:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>Looking over the gallery is a portrait by Cindy Sherman <cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><ccase:Small Caps><cl:9.250000>hon ra<cs:><ccase:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>, constructed from various elements of the artist<0x2019>s face to create an unsettling new character, and nearby hangs a large painting by Tracey Emin <cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><ccase:Small Caps><cl:9.250000>ra<cs:><ccase:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>.

<pstyle:Gallery introduction><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>For Gallery IV, Sir Christopher Le Brun <cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><ccase:Small Caps><cl:9.250000>ppra<cs:><ccase:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000><0x2019>s hang was guided by two principles. The first was colour, the second <0x000A>was scale. 
<cs:><cl:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>On the south and east walls, works by Chris Orr <cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><ccase:Small Caps><cl:9.250000>ra<cs:><ccase:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>, Adam Dant and Jeffrey Dennis share a similar sense of scale and intricate detail. Central to the west wall is a large painting by Dexter Dalwood that was created in Mexico and explores questions around the depiction of history. It is an example of colour field painting that combines the formal language of abstraction with description at a different scale.
<cs:><cl:><praon:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>On the north wall there are works by George Shaw, Edmund de Waal and Mimmo Paladino <cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><ccase:Small Caps><cl:9.250000>hon ra<cs:><ccase:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>. Le Brun<0x2019>s own work <cs:><cl:><ct:Light Italic><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>Phases of the Moon V<ct:><cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000> is part of a series in which he contemplates the seasons and the passing of time, <0x000A>and opposite it, invited artist John Walker<0x2019>s work conveys <0x000A>a powerful physical and sensual presence.
<cs:><cl:><praon:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>Wrapped around the east end of the room are a multitude of prints, ranging from more abstract printmaking on one side with works by Katherine Jones <cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><ccase:Small Caps><cl:9.250000>ra<cs:><ccase:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>, to more narrative and photographic themes on the other.
<cs:><cl:><praon:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>In the centre of the room, Amy Hughes<0x2019>s stoneware pots take inspiration from Greek pottery but are given new life through their painterly decoration and fresh use of colour.
<cs:><cl:><praon:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>The room also features works by the late Timothy Hyman <cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><ccase:Small Caps><cl:9.250000>ra<cs:><ccase:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000> as memorial to the great writer, teacher and figurative painter.

<pstyle:Gallery Roman><ctk:-350> <ctk:><ck:-30>I<ck:>I<cstyle:Works Numbers by numerals>works 1244-1397<cstyle:><cstyle:Gallery Roman\\\: Works>
<cstyle:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>For Richard Wilson<cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><ccase:Small Caps><cl:9.250000> ra<cs:><ccase:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000> and Helen Sear <cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><ccase:Small Caps><cl:9.250000>ra<cs:><ccase:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>, who have collaborated in Gallery II, the connections between other rooms and each other have informed the organic nature of their hang. The pair found they enjoyed not just the process <0x000A>of working together but also the exchange of works with fellow committee members, and finding links between works that they may not have immediately observed.
<cs:><cl:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>The final display is a rich mix of disciplines, where each work is activated by its surroundings, allowing viewers to make connections and find their own narratives; for instance, an assemblage of grey slide mounts by Tonkin Liu sits alongside a canine sculpture and print by Cathie Pilkington <cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><ccase:Small Caps><cl:9.250000>ra<cs:><ccase:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>. 
<cs:><cl:><praon:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>Many other prints in the gallery show the breadth of technology and variety of processes used in contemporary printmaking, including an architectural lightbox print by artistic duo <cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><ccase:All Caps><cl:9.250000>Robocoop<cs:><ccase:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>, intaglio prints by Emma Stibbon <cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><ccase:Small Caps><cl:9.250000>ra<cs:><ccase:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000> demonstrating the force of nature, Emily Allchurch<0x2019>s c-type prints of collaged photographic landscapes, and digital prints of suburban gardens by Anne Desmet <cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><ccase:Small Caps><cl:9.250000>ra<cs:><ccase:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>.
<cs:><cl:><praon:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>Elsewhere, painter and printmaker Frances Gynn uses graphite, charcoal, ink and pastel in her drawing of a stork, while Alison Wilding <cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><ccase:Small Caps><cl:9.250000>ra<cs:><ccase:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000> has constructed her delicate sculptural piece using wood and cast glass.

<pstyle:Gallery 2 deck small><cs:21.000000><cbs:1.000000>L<cs:><cbs:><cs:21.000000><cbs:1.000000><ck:-10>a<cs:><cbs:><ck:><cs:21.000000><cbs:1.000000>rge<cs:><cbs:><cs:17.500000><cl:17.500000><cs:><cl:><cstyle:Works numbers by text>works 1398-1600<0x000A><cstyle:><cs:21.000000><cbs:1.000000>W<cs:><cbs:><cs:21.000000><cbs:1.000000><ck:10>e<cs:><cbs:><ck:><cs:21.000000><cbs:1.000000>ston Room<cs:><cbs:>
<pstyle:Gallery introduction><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>From the beginning, Helen Sear <cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><ccase:Small Caps><cl:9.250000>ra<cs:><ccase:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000> had a clear direction in mind when approaching how to hang the Large Weston Room. Responding to the exhibition theme of Dialogues, Sear chose to focus on the relationship between humans and the natural, non-human world. 
<cs:><cl:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>Des Hughes<0x2019>s wallpaper <cs:><cl:><ct:Light Italic><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>No One Left and No One Came<ct:><cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000> provides the backdrop on one of the main walls and follows a common thread of nature being brought inside. The chain-link fencing suggests tension and a barrier to something unknown. Hung across it are a large group of photographs depicting the countryside, though there are unsettling undertones to the works. Anna Fox and Alison Goldfrapp<0x2019>s collaboration on the series <cs:><cl:><ct:Light Italic><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>Country Girls<ct:><cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000> explores the unease and dark side of country life, while Lottie Davies<0x2019>s photograph of a sleeping man conjures a similar sinister feeling.
<cs:><cl:><praon:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>Nature and the outdoors can also, however, be a refuge, as seen in Anthony Eyton <cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><ccase:Small Caps><cl:9.250000>ra<cs:><ccase:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000><0x2019>s paintings looking out of the door to a cultivated garden, providing a moment of reflection.
<cs:><cl:><praon:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>Within her gallery it was important for Sear to show an expanded version of printmaking and what it looks like in all its forms. For example, Andreas Ruethi<0x2019>s <cs:><cl:><ct:Light Italic><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>Field <0x2013> Tricholoma Vaccinum<ct:><cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000> was developed from a painting inspired by a print, going through many stages before being realised as a hand-tufted carpet.
<cs:><cl:><praon:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>Central to the room are many three-dimensional works <0x000A>made from natural materials, such as Metropolitan Workshop LLP<0x2019>s <cs:><cl:><ct:Light Italic><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>Carpenters Estate, Station Quarter<ct:><cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>, carved from <0x000A>a 115-year-old felled sequoia tree, and Beccy McCray<0x2019>s charred hazel coppice.

<pstyle:Gallery introduction><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>Candice Breitz<0x2019>s <cs:><cl:><ct:Light Italic><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>Factum Tremblay <ct:><cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>is a dual-channel video installation that explores identity and individuality. The work is part of a series that takes its name from Robert Rauschenberg<0x2019>s 1957<cs:><cl:><ct:Light Italic><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000> Factum<ct:><cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000> paintings which, while appearing identical, display subtle differences. Breitz individually filmed sets of identical twins giving in-depth accounts of growing up. The two narratives are interspliced <0x000A>in a way that highlights the divergence and synchronicity <0x000A>of their personalities, perspectives and perceptions.
<cs:><cl:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>Isaac Julien<0x2019>s diptych makes visual reference to the artist<0x2019>s seminal 1989 film<cs:><cl:><ct:Light Italic><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000> Looking for Langston<ct:><cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>, and depicts two figures suspended in time under falling snow, occupying <0x000A>a diasporic dream-space.
<cs:><cl:><praon:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0>In Amak Mahmoodian<0x2019>s project <ct:Light Italic>One Hundred and Twenty Minutes<ct:>, the multidisciplinary artist worked with people in exile to explore the landscapes of their dreams, with this photograph recreating a dream someone had of their sister having twins.<cnxc:Column>

<cstyle:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>This is the fourth time sculptor Richard Wilson <cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><ccase:Small Caps><cl:9.250000>ra<cs:><ccase:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000> has been part of the Summer Exhibition Committee and he has drawn on his previous experience in his hang, applying a few tricks he<0x2019>s learned over the years.
<cs:><cl:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>Knowing he would be hanging the final room <0x2013> Gallery I <0x2013> Wilson says of his approach that he wanted to steer away from the traditional concept of filling walls and instead focus on a smaller number of works, utilising the natural light and airiness of the gallery to give them space to breathe.
<cs:><cl:><praon:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>He also wasn<0x2019>t looking for the larger-scale paintings or sculptures during the selection process and instead sought out whispers rather than shouts <0x2013> pieces that would suit a more domestic environment. Even one of the larger pieces in the room, by invited artist Anne Bean, is made up of smaller component parts, consisting of a series of self-portraits made during a performance where the artist repeated the phrase <0x2018>All communication is translation<0x2019> while thrusting her face <0x000A>into paper and a photocopier.
<cs:><cl:><praon:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>There are groupings of works here that reference architecture, including Humphrey Ocean <cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><ccase:Small Caps><cl:9.250000>ra<cs:><ccase:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000><0x2019>s <cs:><cl:><ct:Light Italic><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>Here and Now<ct:><cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>, and a cluster of more quirky and unusual works that invite the viewer to look at the detail, including Gavin Turk<0x2019>s dustbin. Wilson has also created an uplifting wall of abstract forms that explore the experience of colour, including works by Nigel O<0x2019>Neill and Lawrie Hutcheon.
<cs:><cl:><praon:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0><cl:9.450000>Central to the room are the gilded pelts of 101 rats by collaborative duo Zatorski + Zatorski. As we come out <0x000A>of the pandemic and into an age of AI, the rats gaze <0x000A>at us and ask the question <0x2018>Are you, as humans, the experiment now?<0x2019><cl:><cc:C\=0 M\=100 Y\=0 K\=0><cl:9.450000>

<pstyle:Gallery 2 deck small><cs:21.000000><cbs:1.000000>The <cs:><cbs:><cs:21.000000><cbs:1.000000><ck:-20>L<cs:><cbs:><ck:><cs:21.000000><cbs:1.000000><ck:-30>o<cs:><cbs:><ck:><cs:21.000000><cbs:1.000000>ve<cs:><cbs:><cs:21.000000><cbs:1.000000><ck:-10>l<cs:><cbs:><ck:><cs:21.000000><cbs:1.000000><ck:-20>a<cs:><cbs:><ck:><cs:21.000000><cbs:1.000000><ck:-10>c<cs:><cbs:><ck:><cs:21.000000><cbs:1.000000>e<cs:><cbs:><cs:21.000000>  <cs:><cs:21.000000><cs:><cstyle:Works numbers by text><cs:21.000000><cl:21.000000><cs:><cl:><cs:21.000000><0x000A><cs:><cstyle:><cs:21.000000><cbs:1.000000>C<cs:><cbs:><cs:21.000000><cbs:1.000000><ck:-10>o<cs:><cbs:><ck:><cs:21.000000><cbs:1.000000><ck:-20>u<cs:><cbs:><ck:><cs:21.000000><cbs:1.000000><ck:50>r<cs:><cbs:><ck:><cs:21.000000><cbs:1.000000><ck:30>t<cs:><cbs:><ck:><cs:21.000000><cbs:1.000000><ck:-30>ya<cs:><cbs:><ck:><cs:21.000000><cbs:1.000000>rd<cs:><cbs:><cstyle:Work Number\/Name><clang:English\: UK><ch:Baseline>
<clang:><ch:><cstyle:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>The theme for this year's Summer Exhibition, <cs:><cl:><ct:Light Italic><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>Dialogues<ct:><cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>, set by coordinator Farshid Moussavi RA, sees architecture spread for the first time not only throughout the main galleries, but beyond, creating new conversations with other disciplines rather than being confined to its own space.
<cs:><cl:><praon:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>As part of this initiative, we are pleased to present <cs:><cl:><ct:Light Italic><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>Hr<0x014D>st<ct:><cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000> in the Lovelace Courtyard, a space used by staff and students of the RA Schools. <cs:><cl:><ct:Light Italic><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>Hr<0x014D>st <ct:><cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>is a prototype for a multi-species roost, a sanctuary alongside new hedgerows, trees and water as part of habitat restoration on land that is being rewilded or converted to ecologically managed solar farms. Within the roost are snug boxes for owls, kestrels and sparrows, rough oak crevices for bats, perforated cladding for lacewings and butterflies and a hibernaculum woodpile for other invertebrates, voles and slow worms. Wide overhanging eaves offer shelter for social wasps or migratory birds.
<cs:><cl:><praon:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0><ct:Light Italic><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000>Hr<0x014D>st<ct:><cs:><cl:><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000> was developed from dialogues with species experts, including Edward and Mandy Mayer of Swift Conservation, in collaboration with Price & Myers and BlokBuild, and with support from Passive Purple, True Wool and the Zoological Society of London.
<cs:><cl:><praon:><pstyle:Gallery introduction><praon:0><cs:7.350000><cl:9.250000><cnxc:Column>
